Appendix C - Some of OneShoreline’s Current Projects

OneShoreline is involved in several flooding and sea level rise projects, many already in
progress in various stages when the district was formed. Some of the currently active projects are
summarized below. (For more details on any of these projects, check the OneShoreline website.

Bay Shoreline Project: Burlingame, Millbrae, and San Francisco International Airport

The long-term objective is to raise shoreline and creek bank elevations along 1.6 miles of Bay
shoreline and 1.5 miles of creeks. The project will remove properties from the current FEMA
100-year floodplain and protect them from an additional roughly six feet of sea level rise — a
water level approximately 10 feet above current daily high tide. SFO has a revenue stream and
will protect the airport. OneShoreline’s role is, in part, to coordinate with SFO and the
neighboring cities, as well as to help the cities design and finance their projects.

Bay Shoreline Project: Redwood Shores and San Carlos

Redwood Shores, built upon marshes of San Francisco Bay, is protected by a system of levees.
In 2010, Redwood City raised the height of over three miles of levee surrounding Redwood
Shores. In April 2020, FEMA notified Redwood City that the levees had to be raised again, or a
certain residential area would be designated as a Special Flood Hazard Area requiring
approximately 4,700 households to purchase flood insurance.

Bayfront Canal & Atherton Channel Flood Protection and Ecosystem Restoration Project

The Atherton Channel converges with the Bayfront Canal at the border of Redwood City and
Menlo Park and empties into San Francisco Bay through a tide control structure. High tides keep
the Canal and Channel from draining to the Bay. Even minor rainfall resulted in flooding of
nearby mobile home parks and businesses 40 times over the past 70 years, most recently in 2017.

In 2017, Redwood City, Menlo Park, Atherton, and the County signed an MOU to establish
funding for the design, environmental documentation, and land access agreements. This project
consists of an underground culvert to divert excess flow from the Atherton Channel and the
Bayfront Canal into managed ponds within the Ravenswood Complex of the South Bay Salt
Ponds Restoration Project (SBSPRP). In 2020, OneShoreline assumed the lead role to complete
the project working with the cites and the County.

Colma Creek, San Bruno Creek, Navigable Slough, and nearby areas of the shoreline

Colma Creek, San Bruno Creek, and Navigable Slough are connected waterways within the
cities of South San Francisco and San Bruno that are prone to flooding, especially during high
tide levels in the San Francisco Bay. Colma Creek and San Bruno Creek comprise two of the
three long-standing active flood zones that OneShoreline inherited from the former flood control

90 https://oneshoreline.org/projects/

2020-21 San Mateo County Civil Grand Jury Page 38 of 43

&nbsp;
62 of&nbsp;301


--- page 63 ---
district. OneShoreline has conducted a survey of these studies and potential projects to establish
potential priorities for upcoming projects.

Countywide Flood Early Warning System and Flood Emergency Action Plans

OneShoreline is managing a coordinated, countywide flood emergency preparedness and
response program in collaboration with the Sheriff's Office and the County Office of Emergency
Services. The program upgrades and expands the region’s flood warning system (measurement
and alarms) for emergency responders and the general public. The program includes extensive
public outreach and emergency response personnel training, and the creation of a publicly
accessible flood monitoring webpage.

OneShoreline is also leading the creation of site-specific Flood Emergency Action Plans (EAPs)
to better define and coordinate emergency responsibilities before, during, and after flood events
that cross jurisdictional boundaries for the following areas: Bayfront Canal and Atherton
Channel; Belmont Creek; and Navigable Slough, Colma Creek, and San Bruno Creek.

APPENDIX F - The Army Corps of Engineers Procedures — “Simplified”%®

The sequential steps that are involved in an Army Corps flood control project include:

1.

8.

9.

Non-federal sponsor identifies problem and requests feasibility study. The non-federal
sponsor, typically a city, county, or state, has the legal and financial capability to provide
its share of the project cost.

Congressional authorization to study required, and local sponsor submits letter of intent.
Funds appropriated and study authorized - funds can be requested by Army Corps from
President’s budget, or Congress can appropriate funds.

Study process involves identifying problems, opportunities, objectives, and constraints,
potential alternative plans, and identifies the:

a. National Economic Development (NED) based on maximizing net benefits
relative to costs. Benefits are primarily avoiding economic damages from
flooding. The costs are those of constructing and maintaining the project.

b. National Ecosystem Restoration (NER) alternative.

c. Local sponsors may identify plans beyond the NED or NER and can include those
at their own cost; a levee built higher than what the NED plan included, for
example

Draft integrated feasibility report which includes a draft environmental compliance plan.
Review of the draft by:

a. the public;

b. resource agencies;

c. stakeholders; and

d. Army Corps internal legal, policy and technical.

Recommended Plan includes greater level of design, economic, engineering,
environmental, and other technical details.
Final Feasibility Study Report, including environmental.

a. Recommends project authorization.

Congressional Authorization and construction funding required.

The Army Corps in addition to looking primarily to avoid economic damages from flooding also
evaluates based on:

National Economic Benefit;
Environmental Quality;

Regional Economic Development;
Other Social Effects;

Views of the public;

Federal regulatory agencies;

State regulatory agencies; and
Stakeholders.

95 Grand Jury interview.

Civil works studies and projects compete nationally for congressional appropriations. The Army
Corps also has a set of nine existing authorities under the Continuing Authority Program (CAP)
to plan, design, and construct water resources projects of limited scope and complexity. CAP
studies and projects do not require project-specific authorization from Congress. Potentially
applicable CAP authorities applicable to coastal and fluvial water resources problems include
Section 103 (Beach erosion and storm risk reduction); Section 204 (Beneficial Reuse of Dredge
Material); Section 205 (Flood risk management); and possibly others.

November 2, 2021
Honorable Amarra A. Lee
Judge of the Superior Court
c/o Jenarda Dubois
Hall of Justice
400 County Center
2nd Floor
Redwood City, CA 94063-1655
Re: San Mateo County Civil Grand Jury Report: “San Mateo County: California’s Ground Zero for Sea Level 
Rise.”
Dear Judge Lee:
The Council of the City of San Mateo (City) voted at its public meeting on November 1, 2021, to authorize 
this response to the San Mateo County (SMC) Civil Grand Jury Report: “San Mateo County: California’s 
Ground Zero for Sea Level Rise,” released on August 11, 2021.
Responses to Findings
F1. Sea level rise will seriously damage critical San Mateo County infrastructure and assets unless the 
County and its cities and towns prepare now. 
Response: The City agrees that sea level rise will damage critical infrastructure and assets in SMC. The State 
of California Sea-Level Rise Guidance 2018 Update prepared by the Ocean Protection Council (OPC) issued 
probabilistic projections ranging between 0.9 and 2.7 ft of sea level rise by 2050 and between 2.5 and 10.2 
ft. by the end of the century for San Francisco Bay. The City agrees that the threat is countywide and that 
residents, commuters, and tourists all depend on critical public infrastructure that is vulnerable to sea level 
rise. While the City has a three-mile levee system, this infrastructure will require improvements for it to 
continue to protect residents, property owners, and critical assets (e.g., transportation systems, storm water 
and wastewater facilities, fire stations, medical facilities, utilities, landfills, and schools). As flood protection 
systems are interconnected, the City’s infrastructure will only provide adequate protection if upgraded 
following a coordinated approach with neighboring agencies. Due to their complexity and cost, sea level rise 
projects will require planning, permitting, and funding that will take decades. OneShoreline must therefore 
work with cities and towns to develop a plan that will protect SMC and the City now. 
F2. Sea level rise infrastructure projects can take more than a decade to plan, fund and build.
Response: The City agrees that due to their large scale and cost, sea level rise infrastructure projects will 
take decade(s) to plan, fund, and build. In 2001, for example, the City began taking steps to work with 
CITY OF SAN MATEO 
OFFICE OF THE MAYOR
330 W. 20th Avenue
San Mateo, CA 94403-1338
                                                     www.cityofsanmateo.org  
(650) 522-7040
69 of 301

--- page 70 ---
residents affected by the Federal Emergency Management Agency’s (FEMA’s) special flood hazard area 
designation. After more than two decades of planning and working with multiple regulatory stakeholders, 
the City was able to create an assessment district to fund the necessary flood protection improvements. 
After obtaining permits from seven agencies (California Department of Fish and Wildlife, Regional Water 
Quality Control Board, United States Army Corps of Engineers, State Historic Preservation Office, National 
Marine Fisheries Services, United States Fish and Wildlife Service, and San Francisco Bay Conservation and 
Development Commission) and coordinating with FEMA, the City is now in construction of the North 
Shoreview Levee and Pump Station Improvement Project. This effort consists of levee improvements and 
pump station upgrades that will provide adequate flood protection to the North Shoreview residents and 
will result in the removal of the affected properties from the flood zone. 
Sea level rise projects are larger in scale and cost and require coordination with multiple stakeholders, 
regulatory agencies, and neighboring jurisdictions. With the complex regulatory framework and lack of 
adequate funding, these projects will take decades of effort. The City’s residents will remain vulnerable if 
projects cannot be funded and completed in shorter timeframes. 
F3. Complex federal and state regulations and procedures delay and increase the costs of already 
expensive sea level rise mitigation projects. They need to be revised. 
Response: The City agrees that federal and state regulations and procedures are complex, requiring years of 
coordination to obtain clearance and permitting, often delaying project schedules and increasing costs. The 
regulatory framework needs reform. As noted above, the City’s North Shoreview Levee and Pump Station 
Improvement Project required permits from seven agencies and coordination with FEMA. This effort took 
years of planning, added significant costs, all while the City worked on obtaining property easements and 
developed a financial strategy to secure project funding. 
To provide between 0.9 and 2.7 ft of sea level rise protection by 2050 and 2.5 to 10.2 ft. by the end of the 
century as projected by OPC, flood mitigation projects will have to be expansive. These projects will not only 
have to assess the impacts of severe weather, wet weather flooding, permanent inundation, but also the 
effects of groundwater rise. With the existing complex federal and state regulatory framework, these 
projects will take additional years to permit, extending project timelines and adding costs if there is no 
reform to the regulatory processes. 
F4. Delaying sea level rise projects will increase costs. 
Response: The City agrees that capital infrastructure project costs increase with time. Due to their scale, sea 
level rise projects will be complex, and delays will ultimately result in increased costs. There are also the 
costs associated with no action. In 2019, the California Legislative Analyst Office (LAO) published Preparing 
for Rising Seas which highlighted the cost of no or delayed actions. Planning and investing in efforts to 
mitigate the impacts of sea level rise have a higher benefit-to-cost ratio compared to the costs associated 
with disaster relief. LAO referenced a FEMA sponsored study which “found that for every $1 the federal 
government invested in various types of pre-disaster mitigation activities in recent years, it avoided public 
and private losses totaling $6.” Planning for pre-disaster mitigations through a coordinated and strategic 
approach will likely result in lower costs and more effective protection. 
F5. To remain effective, OneShoreline needs steady, long-term, operational funding. 
70 of 301

--- page 71 ---
Response: The City agrees that all agencies, including OneShoreline, require steady, long-term, operational 
funding to remain effective. The City relies primarily on General Fund revenues for the operation of its 
stormwater systems and implementation of flood control capital projects. The lack of a steady stream has 
been challenging as regulatory requirements and capital needs continue to increase. For the North 
Shoreview Levee and Pump Station Improvement Project, the City relied on the development of an 
assessment district to fund the effort. Earlier this year, the City completed a stormwater funding analysis 
and is assessing the feasibility of establishing a stormwater utility to fund the operating and capital needs. 
Governed by Proposition 218, the stormwater fee will require voter approval. Given the increasing funding 
needs of stormwater management and sea level rise protection, the City believes that coordination on 
capital improvement projects and funding efforts between OneShoreline and the cities and towns is 
required to develop an effective strategy for SMC residents. 
F6. Coordination between neighboring jurisdictions is important to reduce costs and improve the 
effectiveness of a SLR project. 
Response: The City agrees that coordination between neighboring jurisdictions is important to reduce costs 
and improve the effectiveness of SLR projects. As SMC began developing the 2018 Sea Level Rise 
Vulnerability Assessment report, representatives from cities and towns were invited to participate in 
Technical and Policy Advisory Committees, which facilitated input on the project. This type of stakeholder 
engagement was effective and recommended for OneShoreline. Other agencies, such as the City/County 
Association of Governments of San Mateo County, follow a similar structure, with monthly meetings with 
advisory groups that facilitate and support coordination and collaboration between the jurisdictions. Sea 
level rise protection will be highly dependent on a coordinated approach due to the interconnectedness of 
levee networks. A framework that facilitates coordination will result in a more effective strategy to mitigate 
the impacts of sea level rise. 
F7. Competing budget priorities among the entities in a sea level rise project make the projects difficult to 
fund and manage, leading to risk of delays and missed deadlines. 
Response: The City agrees that competing budget priorities make sea level rise projects difficult to manage, 
which can lead to delays. Because sea level rise projects do not have access to a dedicated source of funding, 
they rely on general funds, grants, loans, and are likely to require the development of assessment districts. 
Developing a financial strategy and building public support take time. Cities and towns must prioritize 
projects and balance infrastructure improvements with general fund sources, which alone cannot meet all 
needs.  Due to the setting of priorities and the high cost and complexity of sea level rise projects, these take 
longer to plan and fund.  
F8. Numerous hazardous material sites in the County must be protected from sea level rise flooding. 
Response: The City agrees that hazardous material sites in SMC, such as landfills, contaminated sites, and 
areas with stored chemicals, must be protected from sea level rise flooding. The City has a closed landfill and 
an active wastewater treatment plant that are protected by a levee system and are located in the 500-year 
floodplain as designated by FEMA. Located on Detroit Drive, the wastewater treatment plant is currently 
undergoing an upgrade and expansion. While the new facility will be protected by the levee system, the City 
incorporated a secondary means of protection for the new structures. These will be built with an entry floor 
elevation designed to provide protection from the 100-year base flood and 3.4 ft. of sea level rise. The 3.4 
feet value reflects the high end of the “Likely Range”/ High Emission scenario for year 2100 per OPC’s 
projections. 
71 of 301

--- page 72 ---
The City’s three-mile levee infrastructure will need to be maintained and improved for it to continue to 
protect hazardous material sites, residents, property owners, and critical assets (e.g., transportation 
systems, storm water and wastewater facilities, fire stations, medical facilities, utilities, landfills, and 
schools). As flood protection systems are interconnected, the City’s infrastructure will only provide adequate 
protection if upgraded following a coordinated approach with neighboring agencies. 
F9. Storm surge and sea level rise threaten the County’s wastewater treatment plants affecting everyone 
in the County – even inland County residents. 
Response: The City agrees that storm surges and sea level rise threaten SMC’s wastewater treatment plants, 
affecting many communities including inland residents. The City’s wastewater treatment plant service area 
includes the City, Foster City, Crystal Springs County Sanitation District, and portions of the Town of 
Hillsborough, City of Belmont, and unincorporated areas of SMC. Approximately 150,000 are served by the 
facility, including thousands of inland residents. If the wastewater treatment plant is not adequately 
protected, it would have an impact on all the 150,000 residents that it serves.   
F10. OneShoreline is uniquely positioned to augment San Mateo County’s ability to combat sea level rise 
by its planning, funding, permitting expertise, and guidance. 
Response: The City agrees and supported the formation of OneShoreline to position it to augment SMC’s 
ability to address the impacts of sea level rise. In 2018, the City was part of a Staff Advisory Team (SAT) 
organized by the City/County Association of Government’s Countywide Water Coordination Committee. The 
SAT was tasked with developing a proposal to form a Countywide agency that would increase coordination 
amongst the jurisdictions, avoid the duplication of efforts, and create a unified voice to position SMC and its 
cities to obtain state and federal funding for sea level rise projects. The SAT
completed an intensive six-month engagement and collaboration phase, resulting in the development of the 
proposal for the formation of the Flood and Sea Level Rise Resiliency Agency / OneShoreline. 
We believe that OneShoreline is positioned to meet its mission. 
F11. Destruction of low-cost housing on the Bay and coast by flooding and erosion due to sea level rise will 
further increase inequities in communities such as Belle Haven (Menlo Park), East Palo Alto, Redwood 
City, and Pacifica. 
Response: The City agrees that the destruction of low-cost housing on the Bay and coast by sea level rise will 
further increase inequities in communities such as Belle Haven (Menlo Park), East Palo Alto, Redwood City, 
and Pacifica.
F12. OneShoreline effectively collaborates with the Office of Sustainability and others on public 
engagement campaigns to educate individuals on how sea level rise will affect San Mateo County. 
Response: The City cannot agree or disagree on whether OneShoreline, Office of Sustainability, or others 
have had effective public engagement campaigns.
F13. A loan program to provide cities and town funds for the required preliminary engineering necessary 
to obtain partial state and federal funding for SLR projects would be beneficial. 
72 of 301

--- page 73 ---
Response: The City partially agrees with this finding. While a loan program for sea level rise projects would 
be beneficial, the structure of such program requires further analysis. 
RECOMMENDATIONS
R1. At a public meeting, each city and town council, or board of supervisors should take at least one 
concrete action toward establishing a continuing funding source for OneShoreline, identify that action in 
response to this report, and potentially adopt a resolution expressing support for a parcel tax or property 
tax by June 30, 2022.
Response: This recommendation requires further analysis. The City endorsed the formation of the Flood and 
Sea Level Rise Resiliency Agency / OneShoreline on April 1, 2019 and committed to providing $55,000 a year 
in funding for the first three years. OneShoreline’s mission is to address sea level rise, flooding, coastal 
erosion, and large-scale stormwater improvements through integrated regional planning, design, permitting, 
project implementation, and long-term operations and maintenance. When created, OneShoreline was 
tasked to develop an Integrated Flood and Sea Level Rise Resiliency Investment Plan for the Bayshore and 
the Coastside to address short-term (2050) and long-term (2100) SLR, flooding, and coastal erosion. It is 
critical for this effort to be addressed so projects can be prioritized and financing options assessed. The City 
may consider taking an action to support future funding for OneShoreline upon coordination and review of 
the agency’s plan. 
R2. A coordinated lobbying strategy with participation by the County, by San Mateo County cities and 
towns, by OneShoreline, and by other interested Bay Area cities and counties for federal and state 
regulatory simplification by January 31, 2022. 
Response: The City agrees with this recommendation and is willing to participate in a coordinated lobbying 
strategy with the County, other cities and town, and OneShoreline. 
R4. The County Board of Supervisors and each city and town council, should ensure that their general 
plans regarding SLR protection include transportation and utility infrastructure, schools, public safety 
facilities, and hazardous material sites by March 31, 2022. 
Response: The recommendation has not been implemented but will be implemented in the future. 
Appropriate goals, policies and programs that address sea level rise protection as it pertains to 
transportation and utility infrastructure, schools, public safety facilities, and hazardous material sites will be 
incorporated into the City’s Updated General Plan, which is anticipated for adoption by the end of 2023. The 
Updated General Plan will include timeframes, as appropriate, for implementation.

SUBJECT:
Art in Public Places 2021 Report & Plan – Approval
RECOMMENDATION:
Approve the Art in Public Places 2021 Report & Plan, which includes expenditures to develop a Public Art Master Plan.
BACKGROUND:
The City of San Mateo’s Art in Public Places Ordinance (Ordinance) was approved in July 2005 to promote the development 
of cultural and artistic resources within the community (Chapter 23.60, “Art in Public Places”). The Ordinance requires all 
commercial and multi-family development projects with a building permit valuation of $3 million dollars or more to 
provide on-site public art valued at a half percent (0.5%) of their total valuation, or to pay the equivalent amount as an in-
lieu fee  into the “Art in Public Places Fund” (Fund). The Fund is used for program administration, and for acquisition, 
installation, improvement, and maintenance of the artwork funded by the program. 
Report Highlights 
The 2021 Annual Report & Plan (Report) review is required by the Ordinance and provides information about projects that 
have been completed during the 2021 calendar year, projects that are currently in progress, pipeline projects, and 
administrative activities. At their meeting on September 14, 2021, the Civic Arts Committee (CAC) reviewed the report and 
recommended approval to the City Council.
Completed and Pipeline Projects
There have been two developer projects formally completed this year, the Olympic Wannabes at 540 S. El Camino Real and 
the mosaic entry arch at 1650 S. Delaware. There are three more in process for CAC project approval scheduled for the 
November 8, 2021 meeting, and several in the pipeline for future projects.
Art in Public Places Fund
The Report also describes the Fund’s sources, uses, and current balance. The City’s Comprehensive Fee Study has reviewed 
and updated several of the City’s development impact and in-lieu fees based on current infrastructure costs. Upon final 
approval, the Art in Public Places fee for private development will increase financial support for the breadth of the public 
art activities, public art maintenance, and program administration. Most developers choose to install art in their project, 
however, the One 90 project paid the in-lieu fee this year, which increased the fund balance to approximately $605,000. 
75 of 301

--- page 76 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
Public Art Master Plan
With sixteen years of activity and maturing of the Art in Public Places Program, it may be an appropriate time to plan for 
future activities with the development of a San Mateo Public Art Master Plan. Several jurisdictions within San Mateo 
County, throughout the Bay Area, and in California have either recently completed or are currently implementing a similar 
master planning effort. With the robust addition of public art throughout the City, art has become more visible and 
appreciated by our San Mateo residents, businesses, and visitors. 
Over the years, there have also been several elements identified in the Ordinance that need revision, along with processes 
and procedures that need better alignment with current practices. A Public Art Master Plan would help guide staff 
regarding potential changes to the City's ordinance and procedures. 
This effort could be supported with a contracted consultant to help engage the community to refine the vision, goals, and 
core values of the City’s public art program. Some important elements to address include identifying different types of 
public art the community would like to see, where it is placed, how to acquire the art, and how it is maintained. The plan 
would also revise or develop important administrative guidelines and processes for the art required from private 
developments, public requests, and public art acquisition and/or installation.
There is now a sufficient balance in the Fund to support contracting a consultant to help develop a Public Art Master Plan 
for San Mateo. An estimate for this effort is approximately $50,000-$80,000 based on the proposed Scope of Work in the 
Request for Proposals (RFP). The CAC requests approval from the City Council for this effort.
Equity Art Project
The Equity Art Project has been delayed for reasons that include the contact limitations resulting from the COVID-19 
pandemic. In the meantime, the City Council has directed staff to create a Community Diversity & Equity Taskforce. This 
project has been identified to be a part of the taskforce agenda to provide input to the project that will help move it 
forward.
BUDGET IMPACT:
A program within the Plan contains requests for authorization to develop a Request for Proposals (RFP) for a Public Art 
Master Plan. The Fund’s current budgeted balance is $201,728. The Public Art Master Plan will not require any additional 
appropriation. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
In accordance with CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5), approval of the report is an administrative activity that is not 
subject to CEQA and, in accordance with CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(4), authorization of expenditure from the art in 
public places fund is a financial activity that does not involve commitment to a specific project and is not a project subject 
to CEQA.

OLYMPIC WANNABES 
WESTLAKE OFFICES 
520 S. EL CAMINO REAL, SAN MATEO 
Glenna Goodacre created both the Vietnam 
Women’s Memorial installed in Washington, 
D.C. and the Sacagawea U.S. Dollar image 
first minted in 2000. A description of her 
inspiration for this piece states:  
“The concept of Olympic Wannabes emerged from Goodacre’s observation of 
children enthusiastically watching the 1996 Olympics, specifically gymnastics. She 
thought of all of the children around the world doing cartwheels, while aspiring 
to reach this highest level of athleticism. Thus, the five poses are modeled off the 
various stages of cartwheels, providing a complex and interesting composition. 
She grappled with the challenge of balancing the movement of the five figures 
with an air of light playfulness. She accomplished this through the invisible base 
that is secured underground, making the children appear as though they are 
floating, frozen in the graceful movement of a cartwheel.” 
Olympic Wannabees
Artist: Glenna Goodacre

2 
ART IN PUBLIC PLACES PROGRAM 
DESCRIPTION OF ART IN PUBLIC PLACES ORDINANCE AND PROGRAM 
The City of San Mateo’s Art in Public Places Ordinance (Ordinance) was approved in July 2005 to promote the 
development of cultural and artistic resources within the community (Chapter 23.60, “Art in Public Places”). 
The Ordinance requires all commercial and multi‐family development projects with a building permit valuation 
of $3 million dollars or more to provide on‐site public art valued at a half percent (0.5%) of their total 
valuation, or to pay the equivalent amount into the “Art in Public Places Fund (Fund).” The Fund is used for 
program administration, and for acquisition, installation, improvement and maintenance of the artwork funded 
by the program. The Civic Arts Committee is the advisory body appointed by the City Council to implement the 
Art in Public Places Program (Program) as established by the Ordinance. 
 
ART IN PUBLIC PLACES FUND
 
PROGRAM POLICY 
FUND SOURCES, USES, & FUND BALANCE
The Fund consists of the in‐lieu payments made to the 
program from obligated development projects that 
chose not to install art. As stated in the ordinance, 
these funds are to be used for “acquisition, 
installation, improvement, and maintenance of 
artwork to be displayed in the City, and the 
administration of the Art in Public Places Program.”  
 
Any new art related activities that need expenditure of 
these funds requires a recommendation of approval 
from the Civic Arts Committee to the City Council, and 
the Council’s approval. 
 
The current fund balance in the Art in Public Places 
Capital Improvement Program (CIP) account is 
approximately $605,000. This substantial increase in 
available funds was due to the payment of an in‐lieu 
fee of nearly $400,000 from Pulte Group for the One 
90 project. These funds and any future in‐lieu 
payments will be reserved for future City‐owned 
public art projects, public art maintenance, and 
administrative program activities.  
 
COMPREHENSIVE FEE STUDY  
In fiscal year 2019‐20, the San Mateo City Council 
included a review of the City’s development impact 
fees as a priority in its strategic plan. As a result, in 
2020, the City entered into a contract with two 
consultants, Matrix Consulting Group and Bartle Wells, 
to review and update several of the City’s 
development impact and in‐lieu fees based on current 
infrastructure costs. The Art in Public Places Program 
has been included in that study. City Council is working 
to review and approve the final fee schedule. Their 
decision may result in future fee changes for this 
program. 
 
PUBLIC ART MASTER PLAN 
As noted above, the current Art in Public Places 
ordinance was established in 2005. With the robust 
addition of public art throughout the City since then, art 
has become more visible and appreciated by our San 
Mateo residents and visitors from near and far. 
 
After fifteen plus years, there has been a growth and 
maturing of the program. There have been a variety of 
elements of the ordinance identified that need revision, 
and processes and procedures that need to be in better 
alignment with current practices. 
 
With those learnings and the growing interest in public 
art activities, it would be a timely effort for the City to 
create a Public Art Master Plan. With the engagement 
of the community, this document can help to refine the 
vision, goals, and core values of the City’s public art 
program. It can help identify the different types of art, 
how to acquire art, where it is placed, and how it’s 
cared for.  The plan can also provide important 
administrative guidelines and processes for both the art 
required from private developments as well as public 
acquisition and/or installation.  
 
The fund now has a sufficient balance that can support 
contracting a consultant to help develop the City’s 
Public Art Master Plan. An estimate for this effort is 
approximately $40,000‐$70,000. Staff recommends the 
Civic Arts Committee request approval from the City 
Council to initiate this effort.

EQUITY PUBLIC ART 
PROJECT  
SUPPORTING RACIAL EQUITY  
In July 2020, the City Council adopted a resolution 
reaffirming the City of San Mateo’s commitment to 
racial equality and equity. The Council discussed the 
concept of a community mural or other community art 
project that would reflect and promote racial equity. As 
directed by the Council, the Civic Arts Committee (CAC) 
discussed and approved a proposed process to 
encourage community members to work with the CAC 
for planning and recommendation of an art project to 
Council. It was the desire to have these members help 
define the process that would work best for community 
members to participate. The continuing COVID‐19 
pandemic and some lack of definitive information 
available on time of commitment required were 
resulting in an inability to move forward. This project 
has been identified to be a part of the upcoming 
Community Diversity and Equity Taskforce to provide 
input to the project that will help move it forward.

intent to install art and are working on their proposals. Others are determining the project’s plans to meet the 
public art obligations.

MEETING DATE:
November 01, 2021 
SUBJECT:
US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements Project – Measure A Highway Program Funding
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to authorize an application with the San Mateo County Transportation Authority for Measure A 
Highway Program Funding for the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements project. 
BACKGROUND:
The US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements project (Project) proposes to relocate the southbound US-101 
on and off-ramps from Poplar Avenue to Peninsula Avenue to address safety and operational issues at the existing Poplar 
Avenue ramps. Work began on the Project Approval and Environmental Document (PA/ED) phase in March 2016. 
Following preliminary traffic analysis and extensive public outreach to address community concerns in both the cities of 
San Mateo and Burlingame, the San Mateo City Council reaffirmed their support to continue the PA/ED phase during the 
February 3, 2020 City Council Study Session. 
Due to the increased traffic analysis and outreach scope, additional funding was necessary to complete the PA/ED phase. 
Also, the Project’s original 2016 contract expired and required a new contract with a current schedule of charges for 
personnel that included updated billing rates. These changes resulted in a PA/ED phase cost increase of $2.0 million. City 
Council allocated $600,000 from Measure A funding toward completion of the PA/ED in the fiscal year 2020-21 capital 
improvement budget. The remaining funding required to complete the PA/ED is $1.4 million. 
On August 6, 2021, the San Mateo County Transportation Authority released a Call for Projects for Measure A Highway 
Program grant funds. Measure A is a ballot-approved half-cent sales tax distributed by the San Mateo County 
Transportation Authority for highway and transit improvements pursuant to implement the 2004 Transportation 
Expenditure Plan. 
Staff submitted a grant application for the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements Project to request the 
remaining $1.4 million to complete the PA/ED. As part of the grant application, staff also requested funding for the full 
project design phase in the amount of $4.2 million and to begin the administrative portion of the right-of-way acquisition 
process in the amount of $400,000.
The grant application requires a minimum of 10% matching funds for each phase of work requested. The City’s previously 
allocated $600,000 provides sufficient matching funds for the PA/ED phase grant funds being requested. Table 1 on the 
following page shows the total requested funding and the proposed matching funds to be provided by the City. The 
additional matching funds for design and right-of-way administration must be identified before work starts on these 
phases, which is estimated to occur in fiscal year 2022-23.

Funding for the remainder of right-of-way phase as well as the construction phase will be pursued following completion of 
the PA/ED. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
The grant application requires a minimum 10% match for each phase of work requested in the grant application. Council 
previously allocated $600,000 toward completion of the PA/ED phase in the fiscal year 2020-21 capital improvement 
budget, which suffices as the match for the PA/ED funds being requested. Staff have also proposed an additional $600,000 
in matching funds for the design phase and right-of-way administration phase. These additional matching funds will need 
be identified and allocated prior to the start of work for the design and right-of-way phases, which are estimated to start in 
late 2022.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This grant funding application is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity 
that will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)

CITY OF SAN MATEO
RESOLUTION NO. ___(2021)
SUPPORTING THE US-101/PENINSULA AVENUE INTERCHANGE IMPROVEMENTS PROJECT AND AUTHORIZING 
SUBMITTAL OF AN APPLICATION FOR 2021 MEASURE A AND MEASURE W HIGHWAY PROGRAM FUNDING
WHEREAS, safety and operational issues have been identified at the US-101/E. Poplar Avenue 
southbound on- and off-ramps, and
WHEREAS, relocating the US-101 southbound on- and off-ramps from E. Poplar Avenue to Peninsula 
Avenue will improve safety and circulation in the vicinity of the project, and
WHEREAS, it will cost $7,200,000 to implement the project scope, and
WHEREAS, the City of San Mateo wishes to sponsor completion of the US-101/Peninsula Avenue 
Interchange Improvements Project Approval and Environmental Document, Design, and Right-of-Way 
administration phases, and
WHEREAS, the City of San Mateo seeks $7,200,000 to completion of the US-101/Peninsula Avenue 
Interchange Improvements Project Approval and Environmental Document, Design, and Right-of-Way 
administration phases, and
WHEREAS, on June 7, 1988, the voters of San Mateo County approved a ballot measure to allow the 
collection and distribution by the San Mateo County Transportation Authority (TA) of a half-cent transactions 
and use tax in San Mateo County for 25 years, with the tax revenues to be used for highway and transit 
improvements pursuant to the Transportation Expenditure Plan presented to the voters (Original Measure A); 
and
WHEREAS, on November 2, 2004, the voters of San Mateo County approved the continuation of the 
collection and distribution by the TA of the half-cent transactions and use tax for an additional 25 years to 
implement the 2004 Transportation Expenditure Plan beginning January 1, 2009 (New Measure A); and 
WHEREAS, on November 6, 2018, the voters of San Mateo County approved a ballot measure known as 
"Measure W," which increased the sales tax in San Mateo County by 1/2 percent, and tasked the TA with 
administering four of the five transportation program categories pursuant to the Congestion Relief Plan 
presented to the voters; and 
WHEREAS, the TA issued a Call for Projects for the Measure A and Measure W Highway Program funds 
on August 6, 2021, and
WHEREAS, the TA requires applicants for Measure A and/or Measure W funds to submit a resolution in 
support of the application, in this case for $6,000,000 in Measure A Highway Program funds for completion of 
83 of 301

--- page 84 ---
the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements Project Approval and Environmental Document, 
Design, and Right-of-Way administration phases, and
WHEREAS, the TA also requires applicants to submit a resolution committing to the completion of the 
proposed project scope, in this case completion of the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements 
Project Approval and Environmental Document, Design, and Right-of-Way administration phases, and
WHEREAS, if the TA Board awards Measure A and/or Measure W Highway Program funds to the 
completion of the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements Project Approval and Environmental 
Document, Design, and Right-of-Way administration phases, the TA will requires that the City of San Mateo 
commence work on the completion of the US-101/Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvements Project 
Approval and Environmental Document, Design, and Right-of-Way administration phases within one year of TA 
Board action.
NOW, THEREFORE, BE IT RESOLVED that the City of San Mateo, California: 
1.
Directs staff to submit an application for TA 2021 Measure A and Measure W Highway Program 
funds for $6,000,000 for the PA/ED, design and right-of-way phases for the US-101 Peninsula 
Avenue Interchange Improvement Project.
2.
Authorizes the City Manager to execute a funding agreement with the San Mateo County 
Transportation Authority for the City of San Mateo to receive any Measure A and/or Measure W 
Highway Program funds awarded.
3.
Commits $1,200,000 in City of San Mateo matching funds for the completion of the PA/ED, design 
and right-of-way phases for the US-101 Peninsula Avenue Interchange Improvement Project, if 
awarded the requested TA Measure A and/or Measure W Highway Program funds.
4.
Directs the City of San Mateo to commence work on PA/ED, design and right-of-way phases within 
one year of receiving an award of Measure A and/or Measure W Highway Program Funds.

SUBJECT:
National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System Permit Renewal – Agreement
RECOMMENDATION:
Approve an agreement with Trussell Technologies, Inc. for professional services in support of the reissuance of the 
National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System Permit for the San Mateo/Estero Municipal Improvement District 
Wastewater Treatment Plant in an amount not to exceed $153,490; establish a contingency of $15,350; and authorize the 
Public Works Director to execute the agreement in substantially the form presented and issue change orders within the 
contingency amount. 
BACKGROUND:
Staff requests support from Trussell Technologies, Inc. (Trussell) for the reissuance of National Pollutant Discharge 
Elimination System (NPDES) Permit No. CA0037541 for the San Mateo/Estero Municipal Improvement District Wastewater 
Treatment Plant. The current permit is set to expire June 30, 2023, which requires the City to submit a Report of Waste 
Discharge no later than September 30, 2022. Trussell has teamed with Larry Walker Associates and AECOM to conduct the 
activities described in this scope of work in support of submittal of the Report of Waste Discharge. In addition to the 
standard NPDES permit reissuance activities, this scope of work covers impacts to the permit from both the updated 
wastewater treatment facilities as well as the production of disinfected tertiary recycled water. 
Staff negotiated directly with Trussell as authorized by San Mateo Municipal Code 3.60.040 and the City's Purchasing 
Policy. Trussell has the demonstrated experience and technical understanding to provide the best overall value to the City 
for this very specialized permitting project. Trussell currently contracts with the City for professional services related to 
recycled water production for the Nutrient Removal and Wet Weather Flow Management Upgrade and Expansion Project. 
Trussell’s knowledge of plant processes and working relationship with staff in the Clean Water Program assures the Report 
of Waste Discharge and planned capital improvement work are in alignment. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
Funds are available in the wastewater treatment plant operations budget for this agreement.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This permit renewal is categorically exempt from CEQA as an “existing facility,” because it consists of the operation, repair, 
maintenance, permitting, leasing, licensing, or minor alteration of existing public or private structures, facilities, 
mechanical equipment, or topographical features, involving negligible or no expansion of use. (CEQA Guidelines Section 
15301.)
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.

of CONSULTANT from said obligation.

CITY shall agree upon an estimated nottoexceed cost for such extra work.  In no event shall CONSULTANT be 
paid for additional work that is necessary because of CONSULTANT's errors or oversights.

SECTION 16 - WAIVERS 
 
The waiver by either party of any breach or violation of any term, covenant, or condition of this 
Agreement or of any provisions of any ordinance or law shall not be deemed to be a waiver of such term, 
covenant, condition, ordinance or law or of any subsequent breach or violation of the same or of any other

other term, condition, ordinance, or law.  The subsequent acceptance by either party of any fee or other money 
which may become due hereunder shall not be deemed to be a waiver of any preceding breach or violation by 
the other party of any term, covenant, or condition of this Agreement or of any applicable law or ordinance. 
 
SECTION 17 - COSTS AND ATTORNEYS FEES 
 
Attorney fees in an amount not exceeding $85 per hour per attorney, and in total amount not exceeding 
$5000, shall be recoverable as costs (by the filing of a cost bill) by the prevailing party in any action or actions to 
enforce the provisions of this Agreement.  The above $5000 limit is the total of attorney fees recoverable 
whether in the trial court, appellate court, or otherwise, and regardless of the number of attorneys, trials, 
appeals, or actions.  It is the intent of this Agreement that neither party shall have to pay the other more than 
$5000 for attorney’s fees arising out of an action, or actions to enforce the provisions of this Agreement. 
 
SECTION 18 - NONDISCRIMINATION 
 
CONSULTANT warrants that it is an Equal Opportunity Employer and shall comply with applicable 
regulations governing equal employment opportunity.  Neither CONSULTANT nor any of its subconsultants shall 
discriminate in the employment of any person because of race, color, national origin, ancestry, physical 
handicap, medical condition, marital status, sex, or age, unless based upon a bona fide occupational 
qualification pursuant to the California Fair Employment and Housing Act. 
 
SECTION 19 - MEDIATION 
 
Should any dispute arise out of this Agreement, any party may request that it be submitted to 
mediation.  The parties shall meet in mediation within 30 days of a request.  The mediator shall be agreed to by 
the mediating parties; in the absence of an agreement, the parties shall each submit one name from mediators 
listed by either the American Arbitration Association, the California State Board of Mediation and Conciliation, 
or other agreedupon service.  The mediator shall be selected by a blind draw. 
 
The cost of mediation shall be borne equally by the parties.  Neither party shall be deemed the 
prevailing party.  No party shall be permitted to file a legal action without first meeting in mediation and making 
a good faith attempt to reach a mediated settlement.  The mediation process, once commenced by a meeting 
with the mediator, shall last until agreement is reached by the parties but not more than 60 days, unless the 
maximum time is extended by the parties. 
 
SECTION 20 - LITIGATION 
 
CONSULTANT shall testify at CITY's request if litigation is brought against CITY in connection with 
CONSULTANT's services under this Agreement.  Unless the action is brought by CONSULTANT, or is based upon 
CONSULTANT's wrongdoing, CITY shall compensate CONSULTANT for preparation for testimony, testimony, and 
travel at CONSULTANT's standard hourly rates at the time of actual testimony.

Exhibit A - Scope of Work 
City of San Mateo NPDES Permit Reissuance Support 
October 2021 
The City of San Mateo (the City) has requested support from Trussell Technologies, Inc. 
(Trussell) for the reissuance of NPDES Permit No. CA0037541 for the San Mateo/Estero 
Municipal Improvement District Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP). The current 
permit is set to expire June 30, 2023, which requires the City to submit a Report of Waste 
Discharge (ROWD) no later than September 30, 2022. Trussell has teamed with Larry 
Walker Associates (LWA) and AECOM to conduct the activities described in this scope of 
work.   
In addition to the standard NPDES permit reissuance activities, this scope of work covers 
impacts to the permit from both the updated wastewater treatment facilities (planned to 
come on-line from July 2023 to August 2024) as well as the production of disinfected 
tertiary recycled water. 
Task 1: Prepare Report of Waste Discharge and Supporting 
Documentation 
The City received an NPDES Permit reissuance letter dated August 5, 2021 that described 
the information the Regional Water Quality Control Board (Regional Water Board) needs 
to review prior to reissuing the NPDES permit. In addition to California Form 200, EPA 
Form 2A, and EPA Form 2S, the following information was requested: 
• Facility description 
• Data compilation 
• Monitoring data validity 
• Dilution credits for current discharge 
• Dilution credits for future discharge 
• Bacteria monitoring 
• Cyanide discharges 
• Collection system management 
• Operational changes 
For this task, the project team will develop the forms and compile the requested information 
as listed above. The project team will (1) review and analyze data collected since July 1 
2018 (start of current permit term), (2) complete all forms, (3) prepare a cover letter for the 
ROWD that highlights the requested information, and (4) work with City staff to locate and 
compile information needed to complete the ROWD submittal. The cover letter will 
describe WWTP operations, itemize collection system improvements, identify desired 
changes for the reissued permit, discuss results of the preliminary Reasonable Potential 
Analysis (as needed), and propose strategies to resolve any compliance issues. The cover 
letter will also describe the new recycled water facilities that will produce disinfected tertiary 
recycled water for non-potable reuse. A draft ROWD package will be submitted to the City 
Page 9 of 18
95 of 301

--- page 96 ---
for review, and all requested changes will be completed prior to submittal to the Regional 
Water Board.  
Deliverables:  
• One virtual kickoff meeting with City staff, Trussell, and LWA  
• Submit an information request to support the compilation of the items listed in the 
scope 
• Draft ROWD package including cover letter, completed forms, and supporting 
documentation 
• Final ROWD package submitted to the Regional Water Board 
• Up to two additional conference calls for coordination throughout the task 
Assumptions:  
• Trussell will provide meeting agendas and notes for conference calls, as needed 
• The City will provide all comments on the draft ROWD package in one compiled, 
written document 
• The City will provide comments on the draft ROWD package within three weeks of 
receiving the submittal 
Task 2: Conduct Mixing Zone and Dilution Credit Study 
The project team will conduct an updated mixing zone and dilution credit study in response 
to the Regional Water Board’s request in their reissuance letter. The updated dilution 
analysis will meet the requirements outlined in the letter and will include an assessment of 
both the current discharge scenario (prior to the completion of the plant updates) and future 
discharge scenario (after bringing the new facilities on-line). The study will consider the 
City’s existing outfall to determine initial dilution under relevant discharge conditions 
(current and future), describe mixing zone characteristics, and identify dilution at the edge 
of the mixing zones.  
This study will address the request included in the reissuance letter:  
Dilution Credits for Future Discharge. Provision VI.C.5.d of the current permit 
requires the City to submit with its Report of Waste Discharge a dilution study 
associated with the upgraded wastewater treatment plant using a U.S. EPA-
approved model, such as Visual PLUMES or CORMIX. Estimate the initial dilution 
at Discharge Point 001 for both the peak wet weather and average daily flow with 
the upgraded plant. The initial dilutions should be estimated just after slack tide 
under both the peak wet weather and average daily flow scenarios. The study should 
demonstrate that the discharge receives a minimum initial dilution of at least 10:1.  
The project team will conduct a dilution study to assist the City in obtaining appropriate 
dilution credits that reflect: (1) actual dilution that occurs near the outfall under varying 
conditions of tides, (2) acute and chronic critical conditions for aquatic life criteria (e.g., 
ammonia), as well as chronic toxicity critical conditions, and (3) effective and reasonable 
protection of the beneficial uses in San Francisco Bay in accordance with state and federal 
laws. 
Page 10 of 18
96 of 301

--- page 97 ---
To establish parameters for the near-field dilution analysis, the project team will subcontract 
with AECOM to use its existing Bay model to generate values for ambient currents during 
“normal” and “critically dry year” and provide details on bathymetry near the outfall. 
AECOM will prepare a brief memorandum to summarize the results of their modeling 
effort. 
Deliverables:  
• One virtual conference call to discuss and agree on initial data, assumptions, and 
modeling parameters. The project team will lead the call and present initial 
assumptions.  
• Draft Mixing Zone and Dilution Credit Study report for the City’s review 
• Final Mixing Zone and Dilution Credit Study report that will be submitted to the 
Regional Water Board as part of the ROWD package 
Assumptions:  
• Trussell will provide meeting agendas and notes for the conference call 
• The City will provide all comments on the draft dilution analysis report in one 
compiled, written document 
• The City will provide comments on the draft dilution analysis report within three 
weeks of receiving the submittal 
Task 3: Conduct Preliminary RPA and Calculate Effluent 
Limitations 
The project team will conduct a preliminary reasonable potential analysis (RPA) and 
determine effluent limitations prior to submitting the ROWD package to the Regional 
Water Board. This information will provide the City with a preliminary understanding of 
what effluent limits may be required in the reissued NPDES permit, and will also provide 
the ability to check the Regional Water Board’s separate RPA results. Potential compliance 
issues identified through the preliminary RPA will be noted in the cover letter of the 
ROWD package.  
To conduct the RPA, the project team will compile and analyze effluent and receiving water 
quality data for all regulated constituents. Effluent data will be compiled beginning July 1, 
2018 and receiving water quality data will include all available historical data collected at 
representative locations. The project team will review relevant data characterizing the 
WWTP’s influent, effluent, receiving water, biosolids (if needed), and water supply (if 
needed). In addition to considering the historical water quality, the project team may model 
the projected water qualities associated with the WWTP upgrades, including dry weather 
flows, wet weather flows, and future non-potable recycled water. This analysis of projected 
water quality will be done on an as-needed basis, depending on whether the Regional Water Board is 
interested in seeing the results.   
The validity of the data will be assessed, and potential outliers will be reviewed with the 
City. The project team will then evaluate compliance with the projected effluent limits 
Page 11 of 18
97 of 301

--- page 98 ---
(considering any updates to the anticipated dilution credits from Task 2) and will work with 
the City to develop a compliance strategy for constituents estimated to not comply.  
Deliverables:  
• One virtual conference call to discuss and agree on initial data assumptions and 
assessment approach. The project team will lead the call and present initial 
assumptions.  
• One virtual conference call to review the preliminary RPA and compliance results. 
The project team will lead the call and present initial results. 
• Preliminary RPA and compliance results presented in a memo for City review 
• Final RPA and compliance evaluation results memo for City files. 
Assumptions:  
• Trussell will provide meeting agendas and notes for the conference calls 
• The City will provide all requested data in Excel format within three weeks of 
receiving an information request 
Task 4: Correspond with Regional Water Board and Review 
Permit Iterations 
The project team will coordinate with the City and Regional Water Board throughout the 
project to facilitate the reissuance process. The project team will coordinate with the City 
and will communicate with the Regional Water Board as directed by the City. Specific 
activities that will be conducted as part of the reissuance process will include: 
• Review of the Administrative Draft NPDES Permit and provide comments in track 
changes for Regional Water Board review 
• Review of the Tentative Order and submittal of official comment letter 
• Attendance (as needed) at the Regional Water Board adoption hearing 
• Review Final Order 
An Administrative Draft version of the new permit is provided to the permittee prior to 
development of the Tentative Order and will allow the City to submit comments to the 
Regional Water Board in advance of the public issuance of the Tentative Order. The project 
team will review the Administrative Draft and help the City prepare comments in Track 
Changes that can be submitted to the Regional Water Board. 
After public issuance of the Tentative Order, the project team will review the Order and 
develop an official comment letter. A draft version of the letter will be submitted to the City 
for review and approval prior to submittal to the Regional Water Board. These comments 
will be comprehensive and written for public view, as the official comment letter will 
become public record. After the public comment period, the Regional Water Board will 
develop a Revised Tentative Order. The project team will review the Revised Tentative 
Order to ensure changes have been included as agreed upon by the City and Regional Water 
Board. If there are discrepancies between the City’s comments and the Revised Tentative 
Order and the City would like to pursue additional changes, the project team will help the 
Page 12 of 18
98 of 301

--- page 99 ---
City prepare a presentation and attend the permit adoption hearing (if needed). Finally, the 
project team will review the Final Order to verify all changes made at the hearing were 
implemented correctly. 
Deliverables:  
• Participate in up to two meetings with the Regional Water Board staff to discuss 
permit reissuance requirements, clarify technical documentation (such as the dilution 
study, chronic toxicity results, effluent monitoring results, etc.), and draft permit 
requirements. 
• Edits and embedded comments on the Administrative Draft NPDES Permit 
• Participate in one conference call to discuss the project team’s comments on the 
Administrative Draft 
• Draft and final versions of the official comment letter on the Tentative Order 
• Participate in one conference call to discuss the project team’s comments on the 
Tentative Order 
• Review Revised Tentative Order and (if comments not addressed and the City 
requests assistance) help prepare formal presentation and attend the Permit Adoption 
Hearing for technical support 
• Review the Final Order  
Assumptions:  
• Trussell will provide meeting agendas and notes for the conference calls and 
meetings 
• The conference calls to review the Administrative Draft and Tentative Order will last 
no longer than two hours 
• The City will provide written comments on the draft official comment letter no more 
than two weeks after submittal  
Task 5: Develop 5-Year Compliance Calendar 
The project team will compile all the compliance requirements from the newly adopted 
permit into a compliance calendar and prepare a summary of monitoring requirements for 
the City. This will ensure the City is aware of any new requirements of the reissued permit 
and knows how and when to conduct activities and submit documentation to show 
compliance with the requirements. This compliance calendar will cover the entire five-year 
term of the reissued permit.   
To provide this compliance calendar in advance of the Final Order, the first draft will be 
developed based on the Tentative Order. The final compliance calendar will incorporate any 
changes requested by the City and will be based on the Final Order. 
Deliverables:  
• Draft 5-year compliance calendar and summary of monitoring requirements (based 
on Tentative Order) 
• Final 5-year compliance calendar and summary of monitoring requirements (based 
on Final Order) 
Page 13 of 18
99 of 301

--- page 100 ---
Assumptions:  
• The City will provide written comments within three weeks of receiving the draft 
compliance calendar 
Task 6: Team Coordination and Project Management 
Trussell will provide project management activities throughout the project to facilitate 
adherence to the schedule and budget proposed in this Scope of Work. Project management 
activities will include all necessary coordination between Trussell, LWA, AECOM, and the 
City. This includes coordination meetings and monthly invoicing activities.   
Deliverables:  
• Up to four additional conference calls with the City and LWA as needed throughout 
the project 
• Coordination among project team and the City 
• Monthly invoicing 
Assumptions:  
• Activities described for this task will be conducted throughout the total contract 
duration. If the contract duration increases, additional budget may be necessary to 
continue providing these services. 
• The conference calls will last no longer than one hour. Prior to the meeting, Trussell 
will submit an agenda, and meeting minutes will be made available by Trussell after 
the meeting. 
Schedule 
This scope of work includes the following key milestones: 
• By March 1, 2022: Conduct mixing zone modeling, determine applicable dilution 
credits, and complete final report  
• By August 1, 2022: Conduct preliminary RPA and calculate effluent limitations  
• By September 1, 2022: Submit ROWD package 
• Activities performed in 2023 (depending on Regional Water Board schedule): 
o Review Administrative Draft NPDES Permit and submit comments 
o Review Tentative Order and submit comments 
o Attend permit adoption hearing, if needed 
o Review Final Order 
o Prepare 5-year compliance calendar and summary of monitoring 
requirements 
All work associated with this scope is expected to be completed by December 31, 2023. 
Trussell Tech’s proposed engineering consultant fee for the scope of work described above is 
$153,490. The following figures provide additional schedule and budget details.

EXHIBIT C 
 
INSURANCE REQUIREMENTS 
 
MINIMUM SCOPE OF INSURANCE  
Coverage shall be at least as broad as: 
 
1. Commercial General Liability (CGL):  Insurance Services Office (ISO) Form CG 00 01 12 07 
covering CGL on an “occurrence” basis, including products-completed operations, personal & 
advertising injury, with limits no less than $2,000,000 per occurrence. If a general aggregate 
limit applies, either the general aggregate limit shall apply separately to this project/location or 
the general aggregate limit shall be twice the required occurrence limit. 
 
2. Automobile Liability:  ISO Form Number CA 00 01 covering any auto (Code 1), or if 
CONSULTANT has no owned autos, hired, (Code 8) and non-owned autos (Code 9), with limit no 
less than $1,000,000 per accident for bodily injury and property damage. 
 
3. Workers’ Compensation: as required by the State of California, with Statutory Limits, and 
Employer’s Liability Insurance with limit of no less than $1,000,000 per accident for bodily 
injury or disease.  
 
4. Professional Liability (Errors and Omissions): Insurance appropriate to the CONSULTANT’s 
profession, with limit no less than $1,000,000 per occurrence or claim, $2,000,000 aggregate 
 
If the CONSULTANT maintains higher limits than the minimums shown above, the City requires and 
shall be entitled to coverage for the higher limits maintained by the CONSULTANT. 
 
Other Insurance Provisions 
The insurance policies are to contain, or be endorsed to contain, the following provisions: 
 
Additional Insured Status 
The City, its elected and appointed officials, employees, and agents are to be covered as insureds on 
the auto policy for liability arising out of automobiles owned, leased, hired or borrowed by or on behalf 
of the CONSULTANT; and on the CGL policy with respect to liability arising out of work or operations 
performed by or on behalf of the CONSULTANT including materials, parts or equipment furnished in 
connection with such work or operations. General liability coverage can be provided in the form of an 
endorsement to the CONSULTANT’s insurance (at least as broad as ISO Form CG 20 10, 11 85 or both 
CG 20 10 and CG 20 37 forms if later revisions used). 
 
Primary Coverage 
For any claims related to this contract, the CONSULTANT’s insurance coverage shall be primary 
insurance as respects the City, its elected and appointed officials, employees, and agents. Any 
insurance or self-insurance maintained by the City, its elected and appointed officials, employees, or 
agents shall be excess of the CONSULTANT’s insurance and shall not contribute with it. 
 
Page 17 of 18
103 of 301

--- page 104 ---
Notice of Cancellation 
Each insurance policy required above shall provide that coverage shall not be canceled, except after 
thirty (30) days’ prior written notice (10 days for non-payment) has been given to the City. 
 
Waiver of Subrogation 
CONSULTANT hereby grants to City a waiver of any right to subrogation which any insurer of said 
CONSULTANT may acquire against the City by virtue of the payment of any loss under such insurance.  
CONSULTANT agrees to obtain any endorsement that may be necessary to effect this waiver of 
subrogation, but this provision applies regardless of whether or not the City has received a waiver of 
subrogation endorsement from the insurer.   
 
Deductibles and Self-Insured Retentions 
Any deductibles or self-insured retentions must be declared to and approved by the City. The City may 
require the CONSULTANT to purchase coverage with a lower deductible or retention or provide proof 
of ability to pay losses and related investigations, claim administration, and defense expenses within 
the retention.   
 
Acceptability of Insurers 
Insurance is to be placed with insurers with a current A.M. Best’s rating of no less than A:VII, unless 
otherwise acceptable to the City. 
 
Verification of Coverage 
CONSULTANT shall furnish the City with original certificates and amendatory endorsements or copies 
of the applicable policy language effecting coverage required by this clause.  All certificates and 
endorsements are to be received and approved by the City before work commences.  However, failure 
to obtain the required documents prior to the work beginning shall not waive the CONSULTANT’s 
obligation to provide them.  The City reserves the right to require complete, certified copies of all 
required insurance policies, including endorsements required by these specifications, at any time.

SUBJECT:
Wastewater Treatment Plant On-Call Engineering Services – Amendments
RECOMMENDATION:
Approve amendments to four different agreements for on-call professional design services for the wastewater treatment 
plant rehabilitation projects with Hazen and Sawyer D.P.C., dba Hazen & Sawyer, HDR Engineering, Inc., Kennedy/Jenks 
Consultants, Inc. and West Yost Associates, Inc. for two-year extensions at no additional cost; and authorize the Public 
Works Director to execute the amendments in substantially the form presented and issue project-specific task orders 
under these agreements.
BACKGROUND:
The Clean Water Program (CWP) includes significant infrastructure upgrades at the wastewater treatment plant (WWTP). 
The WWTP is an aging facility, and the CWP consists of a variety of tasks to further the design and construction of capital 
improvement projects to rehabilitate and upgrade existing facilities to maintain reliable plant operations and meet 
regulatory permit requirements. 
In December 2017, four engineering consulting firms (Hazen and Sawyer, HDR Engineering, Inc., Kennedy/Jenks 
Consultants, Inc. and West Yost Associates, Inc.) were each awarded agreements to assist the City in designing 
rehabilitations of the existing WWTP. Scope of work for each of the consultants included alternatives analysis, field 
investigations, engineering design, development of bid documents (plans, details, specifications, and construction cost 
estimates), and engineering services during construction for various WWTP projects. City Council approved amendments 
on October 7, 2019, to extend the agreements with all four firms for an additional two-years, with the agreements set to 
expire in December 2021. 
Currently, work at the WWTP is anticipated to extend beyond the current December 2021 expiration date. The remaining 
work includes Clean Water Program efforts associated with the Annual Major Components Project and Immediate Action 
Projects. Staff recommends extending the term of each agreement by two years to allow these professional design 
services to continue through the completion of these rehabilitation projects. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
These amendments revise the term of the agreements at no additional cost or budget impact. The original agreements, 
each with a not-to-exceed amount of $300,000, have sufficient remaining balances to fund the remainder of activities 
through the term extensions.

ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
These amendments are exempt from CEQA review because it can be seen with certainty that there is no possibility that 
amending engineering design services agreements may have a significant effect on the environment. (CEQA Guidelines 
section 15061(b)(3).) The resulting sewer rehabilitation projects are also categorically exempt from CEQA as “existing 
facilities” since it consists of the operation, repair, maintenance, permitting, leasing, licensing or minor alteration of 
existing public structures, facilities, or mechanical equipment involving negligible or no expansion of existing or former 
use. (CEQA Guidelines section 15301.)

SUBJECT:
Peninsula Traffic Congestion Relief Alliance – Joint Powers Agreement Amendment 
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to approve the second amendment to and restatement of the Joint Powers agreement establishing the 
Peninsula Traffic Congestion Relief Alliance and authorize the Mayor to execute the amendment in substantially the form 
presented.
BACKGROUND:
The Peninsula Traffic Congestion Relief Alliance ("the Alliance") was established in 2000 to assist public and private sectors 
in the County of San Mateo with the reduction of traffic congestion and air pollution. The Alliance was originally created 
through a Joint Powers Agreement (“JPA”) among fifteen cities in the County. The City of San Mateo is an active member 
of the Alliance. 
In 2005, the County of San Mateo along with the Towns of Atherton and Hillsborough were added as members of the 
Alliance. To add the new members, all 15 then-current members, together with the 3 new members, approved and 
executed a first amendment to and restatement of the JPA establishing the Alliance. The amended JPA provided for the 
addition of the new members and revised and made more flexible the committee structure of the Alliance. 
The City of Menlo Park and the Town of Portola Valley have expressed interest in joining the Alliance, and the Town of 
Woodside is considering membership as well. To facilitate adding these new members, the Alliance Board of Directors 
reviewed the proposed second amendment to and restatement of the JPA establishing the Alliance and approved it for 
presentation to both current and new members for approval and execution at its meeting on September 16, 2021. In 
addition to increasing the membership of the Alliance, this second amendment (1) updates and refreshes the first section 
of the original JPA under "Purpose" to reflect the Alliance's recently adopted Mission and Vision Statements as set forth in 
its Strategic Plan and (2) clarifies that the Alliance has developed a new brand and has become known to the public as 
"Commute.org." 
Staff recommends City Council adopt the resolution approving the amendment and authorize the Mayor to sign the 
amendment on the City's behalf to include the three remaining non-member cities in the County in the Alliance.
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no fiscal impact associated with approving this resolution.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
The adoption of a resolution is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that 
will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)

SECOND AMENDMENT TO AND RESTATEMENT OF THE JOINT POWERS AGREEMENT ESTABLISHING THE 
PENINSULA TRAFFIC CONGESTION RELIEF ALLIANCE AND AUTHORIZING EXECUTION OF SAME
WHEREAS, the Peninsula Traffic Congestion Relief Alliance (“Alliance”) was established in 2000 through a 
Joint Powers Agreement (“JPA”) executed by fifteen cities in the County of San Mateo, including the City of San 
Mateo, to assist the public and private sectors in achieving traffic congestion relief; and
WHEREAS, in 2005, the JPA was amended to include the County of San Mateo, and the Towns of 
Atherton and Hillsborough as members of the Alliance, bringing the total membership to 18 agencies, all of 
which approved and executed the first amendment to and restatement of the Joint Powers Agreement; and
WHEREAS, the City of Menlo Park and the Town of Portola Valley now desire to join the Alliance and the 
Town of Woodside may consider membership as well; and
WHEREAS, adding members to the Alliance requires all of the current members as well as the new 
members to approve and execute a second amendment of the Joint Powers Agreement; and
WHEREAS, in addition to expanding the membership of the Alliance, the proposed second amendment 
to the Joint Powers Agreement takes the opportunity to update and refresh the JPA by (1) revising the first 
section under "Purpose" to reflect the Alliance's recently adopted Mission and Vision Statements as set forth in 
its Strategic Plan and (2) clarifying that the Alliance has developed a new brand and has become known to the 
public as "Commute.org"; and
WHEREAS, at its meeting on September 16, 2021, the Alliance Board of Directors reviewed the proposed 
second amendment to and restatement of the Joint Powers Agreement establishing the Peninsula Traffic 
Congestion Relief Alliance that incorporates the changes recited above and approved this second amendment 
for presentation to the San Mateo County Board of Supervisors and the city councils of current and new 
members for approval and execution of same; and
WHEREAS, adoption of this resolution approving the second amendment to and restatement of the Joint 
Powers Agreement is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that 
will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)); 
NOW, THEREFORE, THE CITY COUNCIL OF THE CITY OF SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA, HEREBY RESOLVES 
that: 
1.
The second amendment to and restatement of the Joint Powers Agreement establishing the Peninsula 
Traffic Congestion Relief Alliance is approved.
2.
The Mayor is authorized to execute the amendment and the City Clerk is authorized to attest to the 
document.
